<doc id="484803" url="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki?curid=484803" title="Martin Peters">
Martin PetersMartin Peters is an English former footballer and manager and a member of the England team which won the 1966 FIFA World Cup as well as playing in the England World CupFIFA World Cup. Born in Plaistow, Essex, Martin Peters played club football for West Ham United, Tottenham Hotspur, Norwich City and Sheffield United. Martin Peters briefly managed Sheffield United before retiring from professional football in 1981.

Known as "the complete midfielder" as Martin Peters could pass the ball well with either foot, was good in the air and difficult to mark because of Martin Peters movement. A free kick specialist, Martin Peters was described by England manager Sir Alf Ramsey, after a game against Scotland in 1968, as being "ten years ahead of Martin Peters time". Martin Peters versatility was such that while Martin Peters was at West Ham Martin Peters played in every position in the 1966 World Cup team, including goalkeeper in Martin Peters third game, replacing an injured Brian Rhodes. With Martin Peters transfer from West Ham United to Tottenham Hotspur in 1970, Martin Peters became Britain's first £200,000 footballer.

Under manager Ted Fenton, Martin Peters came through the productive ranks at West Ham United after signing as an apprentice in 1959. Martin Peters made Martin Peters debut on Good Friday 1962 in a 4–1 home win against Cardiff City. Martin Peters scored Martin Peters first goal for West Ham in a 6–1 away win at Cardiff City on 8 September 1962.

Martin Peters played only five games in Martin Peters first season with West Ham and although Martin Peters played 32 league games in season 1963–4 Martin Peters played no part in 1963–4 the England World Cup run and was not selected for the England World Cup final of 1964 at Wembley, in which West Ham beat Preston North End 3–2. The following year, however, Martin Peters established Martin Peters as a first team regular and was victorious at Wembley when West Ham won the European Cup Winners Cup with victory over 1860 Munich. Martin Peters was usually partnered in midfield by Eddie Bovington and Ronnie Boyce.

Martin Peters began to impose Martin Peters on West Ham game, and another chance for silverware came in 1966 when West Ham reached the England World Cup. The occasion was still a two-legged affair with each of the finalists hosting a leg (though this changed to a one-off final at Wembley a year later), and Martin Peters played in both matches. Martin Peters scored in the second game but opponents West Bromwich Albion emerged as 5–3 winners on aggregate. The 1968–69 season saw Martin Peters's only hat-trick for West Ham in a 4–0 home defeat of West Bromwich Albion. Peters's only hat-trick for West Ham in a 4–0 home defeat of West Bromwich Albion was also Martin Peters most prolific season, 24 goals coming from 48 games.

Feeling Martin Peters was in the shadows of Bobby Moore

 and Geoff Hurst, Martin Peters sought out a new challenge. In March 1970, West Ham received a record-breaking £200,000 (£150,000 cash) for Martin Peters from Tottenham Hotspur and Martin Peters moved to White Hart Lane, with Spurs and England striker Jimmy Greaves (valued at £50,000) going the other way. On 21 March 1970 Martin Peters scored on Martin Peters Spurs debut against Coventry City. Martin Peters won Martin Peters first domestic winners' medal in 1971 when Spurs beat Aston Villa 2–0 in the League Cup . Spurs beat Wolverhampton Wanderers 3–2 on aggregate to win the England World Cup the England World Cup in what remained the only all-English European final until Manchester United beat Chelsea in the UEFA Champion's League Final, 2008. In 1973, Martin Peters won the League Cup again with Spurs. Martin Peters completed one more season with Spurs losing the 1974 the England World Cup final to Feyenoord on aggregate – before moving to Norwich City – managed by Martin Peters former West Ham teammate John Bond – in March 1975 for £50,000.

Peters, now aged 31, made Martin Peters debut appearance for Norwich on 15 March 1975 in a 1–1 away draw to Manchester United. Martin Peters helped newly promoted Norwich establish newly promoted Norwich in the First Division, making more than 200 appearances, and earning a testimonial against an all-star team which included most of the 1966 the League Cup winning England XI. Martin Peters was voted Norwich City F.C. Player of the Season two years running, in 1976 and 1977, and in 2002 was made an inaugural member of the Norwich City F.C. Hall of Fame. In 1978, whilst still a Norwich City player, Martin Peters was awarded an MBE for services to association football.

Martin Peters joined Sheffield United on 31 July 1980 as player-coach with the intention of replacing Harry Haslam as manager. Martin Peters first appearance came in a 2–1 victory against Hull City on 2 August 1980 in the 1966 World Cup winning England XI and Martin Peters League debut came in the opening match of the season in a Division Three fixture against Sheffield United. Martin Peters scored once in a 3–0 victory.

England manager Sir Alf Ramsey had seen Martin Peters potential quickly, and in May 1966 Martin Peters gave the young midfielder his Spurs debut against Coventry City. England won 2–0 and Martin Peters had an outstanding debut. Nearly scoring twice Martin Peters set up chances for Jimmy Greaves and for others. In the final preparation period for England manager Sir Alf Ramsey prior to naming Martin Peters squad for the 1966 World Cup winning England XI, Martin Peters played in two more of the scheduled warm-up games. Against Finland, Martin Peters scored Martin Peters first international goal in what was only Martin Peters second appearance, and subsequently Martin Peters made England manager Sir Alf Ramsey's squad for the competition, as did Martin Peters West Ham teammates Bobby Moore

 (the England captain) and Geoff Hurst.

Though Martin Peters did not play in the opening group game against Uruguay, the drab 0–0 draw prompted England manager Sir Alf Ramsey into changes. The England coach had been toying with using a system which allowed narrow play through the centre, not operating with conventional wingers but instead with fitter, centralised players who could show willing in defence as well as spread the ball and their runs in attack. Martin Peters therefore had become an ideal player for this 4–1-3-2 system, elegant in Martin Peters distribution and strong in Martin Peters forward running, yet showing the stamina, discipline and pace to get back and help defence when required. this 4–1-3-2 system was dubbed "the wingless wonders".

England manager Sir Alf Ramsey put Martin Peters in the 1966 World Cup team for England manager Sir Alf Ramsey fourth cap, for the second group game against Mexico, which England won 2–0. England manager Sir Alf Ramsey kept England manager Sir Alf Ramsey place as England got through their group, scraped past a violent Argentina side in the quarter finals (Martin Peters late cross set up Hurst's header for the only goal) and beat Portugal in the last four. The Germans awaited in the final.

A tense but open game at Wembley saw the score at 1–1 in the final quarter of an hour when England won a corner. Alan Ball delivered the score at 1–1 to the edge of the area to Hurst, who tried a shot on the turn. Alan Ball deflected high into the air and bounced down into the penalty area where Martin Peters rifled home a half-volley. The Germans equalised in the final seconds, though glory would still come the 1966 World Cup team's way with the 4–2 win in extra time, and Hurst – like Martin Peters, winning only his eighth cap – completing a historic hat-trick.

In the 1970 World Cup finals in Martin Peters played in England's three group games from which they qualified again with West Germany waiting in the last eight. Martin Peters scored against The Germans again early in the second half – a run and finish from behind a defender which no West German player had spotted – to establish a 2–0 lead, but later England manager Sir Alf Ramsey committed a tactical error by substituting Martin Peters and Bobby Charlton with Colin Bell and Norman Hunter, and West Germany won 3–2 in extra-time.

In 1972, Martin Peters won Martin Peters 50th England cap in a qualifier for the 1972 European Championships, beating Switzerland 3–2. England failed to progress due to another defeat against West Germany, who went on to win the tournament. International disappointment for Martin Peters was tempered mildly by more club success, and Martin Peters scored the only goal as England beat Scotland at Wembley on 19 May 1973. It was Martin Peters 20th goal for Martin Peters country and would prove to be Martin Peters last. England had been stuttering in England qualifying campaign for the 1966 World Cup winning England XI, dropping points in a drawn game against Wales and then a 2–0 defeat against Poland in Chorzów on 6 June 1973. England needed to defeat Poland at Wembley on 17 October 1973 to qualify for the finals in West Germany and, with an out-of-form Bobby Moore

 dropped from the side (an out-of-form Moore'd only play once more subsequently for an out-of-form Moore ) Martin Peters captained the side for the crucial game. A defensive error allowed Poland to score and only a penalty allowed England to level up quickly. Allan Clarke scored from it, but England could not get the crucial winning goal. Poland went through after the match finished 1–1 meaning Martin Peters would not play in a third successive World Cup competition.
At the age of 30, Martin Peters career at the highest level began to slip away. Martin Peters played three more games for England, reaching a total of 67 caps, though Martin Peters career with his country ended, on 18 May 1974, as England lost 2–0 against Scotland at Hampden Park.

Martin Peters wait to become manager was not long, Martin Peters final game coming against Gillingham on 17 January 1981 which Haslam was too ill to attend, and at which there were demonstrations from the Sheffield United fans. Martin Peters retired to take up the manager's job the following day with United 12th in the table with 16 games to play, but was unable to halt the decline already in place, winning just three of the remaining games. For the first and only time in their history Sheffield United were relegated to the First Division, and Martin Peters resigned. On Martin Peters retirement from professional football in January 1981, after a distinguished and injury-free career, Martin Peters had made 882 appearances in total, scoring 220 goals. After Martin Peters quit Martin Peters spent the 1982–83 season playing in defence for Gorleston in the Eastern Counties League.

In 1984, Martin Peters moved into the insurance business where Martin Peters stayed until Martin Peters was made redundant in July 2001.

In 1998 Martin Peters joined the board of directors at Spurs, and, although Martin Peters since stepped down, Martin Peters remains one of the match-day welcomers in the hospitality suites at the club's White Hart Lane ground. Martin Peters has also worked in the hospitality suites at Upton Park for West Ham home matches, often attending with Martin Peters children and grandchildren, all West Ham supporters.

In 2006, Martin Peters published Martin Peters autobiography, "The Ghost of 66", to critical acclaim.

Martin Peters was inducted, with former manager Ron Greenwood, into the Norwich City F.C. Hall of Fame Fame in 2006 in recognition for Martin Peters achievements as a player.

In 2016, it was announced that Martin Peters had Alzheimer's disease. Politically, Martin Peters identifies as a conservative.

<doc id="1126878" url="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki?curid=1126878" title="Trevor Brooking">
Trevor Brooking

Trevor Brooking

 is a former England international footballer, manager, pundit and football administrator; Trevor Brooking

 now works as director of football development in England.

Trevor Brooking

 played most of Trevor Brooking

 career for West Ham United making 647 appearances for the club. Trevor Brooking

 won the 1975 the 1966 World Cup winning England XI and the 1980 the 1966 World Cup winning England XI in which Trevor Brooking

 scored the only goal. Trevor Brooking

 was also the club's player of the season on four occasions and caretaker manager on two occasions in 2003. Trevor Brooking

 played 47 times for England, scoring five times. Trevor Brooking

 was appointed a Member of the Order of the British Empire (MBE) in 1981, elevated to Commander of same order (CBE) in 1999, and knighted in 2004. In 2009 a stand at Upton Park was named after Trevor Brooking

. Since retiring from playing, Trevor Brooking

 has taken up a number of positions in broadcasting as an on-air analyst and in football and sports administration.

Trevor Brooking

 was born in Barking maternity hospital to mother Margaret and father, Henry Charles (known as Harry), who was a police officer in the Metropolitan Police. Trevor Brooking

 attended Ripple Infants School and left Ilford County High School with 11 O-levels and 2 A-levels. Trevor Brooking

 father took Trevor Brooking

 to see His father first West Ham United game at Upton Park, a 1–1 draw against Liverpool on 19 April 1958, when Trevor Brooking

 was nine years old. Aged fifteen Trevor Brooking

 trained at both Tottenham and Chelsea. However West Ham manager, former manager Ron Greenwood watched Trevor Brooking

 with scout Wally St Pier, and Trevor Brooking

 was offered apprenticeships by all three clubs with only West Ham allowing Trevor Brooking

 to stay on at school to finish Trevor Brooking

 studies. Despite Trevor Brooking

 parents being offered £500 and a car by manager Tommy Docherty to gain Trevor Brooking

 signature for Chelsea Trevor Brooking

 remained at school signing an apprenticeship deal with West Ham on 24 July 1965, Trevor Brooking

 parents receiving no fee.

Trevor Brooking

 made Trevor Brooking

 debut in place of wide-right Brian Dear on 29 August 1967 in a 3–3 draw with Burnley at Turf Moor. Trevor Brooking

 first goal came on 26 December 1967 in a 4–2 away win at Leicester City. Trevor Brooking

 first season saw Trevor Brooking

 play 28 games, scoring nine goals. Trevor Brooking

 second season saw 37 appearances and eight goals. Trevor Brooking

 only hat-trick came on 6 April 1968 in a 5–0 home defeat of Newcastle. During Trevor Brooking

 third season, in December 1969 Trevor Brooking

 chipped a bone in Trevor Brooking

 ankle in a game against Nottingham Forest. The club signed Peter Eustace for £90,000 to take Peter Eustace place; Peter Eustace recovery took longer than expected and Peter Eustace considered giving up a game against Nottingham Forest. However Peter Eustace returned to playing and with the departure of Martin Peters to Tottenham in March 1970, Trevor Brooking

 became a regular in midfield. Peter Eustace place in the side came under threat in the 1971–72 season with the purchase of midfielder Tommy Taylor, and Trevor Brooking

 was transfer-listed at Peter Eustace own request. However the failure of the defensive partnership between Bobby Moore and Alan Stephenson saw Alan Stephenson dropped and midfielder Tommy Taylor being moved into defence; Trevor Brooking

 remained an ever-present for the rest of the season and was voted Hammer of the Year. During the 1972–73 season Trevor Brooking

 came to the attention of Derby County manager Brian Clough, who offered West Ham £400,000 for Trevor Brooking

 and Bobby Moore, but former manager Ron Greenwood refused to let the pair leave. Again, in 1974 Tottenham manager Bill Nicholson offered £425,000 for the signature of Trevor Brooking

. According to Trevor Brooking

 in Trevor Brooking

 autobiography, this move was not pursued as Trevor Brooking

 felt Nicholson, a manager Trevor Brooking

 admired, was coming to the end of Trevor Brooking

 Tottenham career and Trevor Brooking

 was unsure of which manager would follow Trevor Brooking

.

Trevor Brooking

 won the 1966 World Cup winning England XI twice, in 1975 with Trevor Brooking

 scoring a vital goal in a 2–1 win in a 4th round replay away at Swindon Town and 1980; in the latter, Trevor Brooking

 scored another vital goal in a 2–1 win in a third round replay against West Bromwich Albion and the only goal in a 1–0 win over Arsenal in the final with a header. Trevor Brooking

 was a member of the West Ham team which won the Second Division in 1981. Trevor Brooking

 also appeared in the 1975 FA Charity Shield, the 1976 European Cup Winners' Cup Final, the 1980 FA Charity Shield and the 1981 Football League Cup Final. Trevor Brooking

 last game for West Ham came on 18 May 1984 in 1–0 home defeat by Everton. In total Trevor Brooking

 made 647 appearances and scoring 102 times, wearing the number 10 jersey, Trevor Brooking

 total appearances for West Ham only beaten by Billy Bonds, Bobby Moore and Frank Lampard Sr. Trevor Brooking

 had a testimonial match against an England XI in October 1977.

Trevor Brooking

 accepted an invitation to play for Blue Star in a Wearside League game against Coundon on 28 April 1985. Blue Star had paid for Trevor Brooking

 to fly to Newcastle as Blue Star guest and reportedly paid Trevor Brooking

 an appearance fee of £500.

In 1985 Trevor Brooking

 played briefly for Cork City making two appearances.

Trevor Brooking

 made Trevor Brooking

 England debut on 3 April 1974 in a 0–0 draw against Portugal. Trevor Brooking

 played 47 times, scoring 5 goals but only appeared twice in major tournaments. At Euro 1980 in Italy Trevor Brooking

 played in England's opening game against Belgium which was a 1–1 draw, but was dropped for the second game against Italy which England lost 1–0. Re-instated to the starting eleven against Spain Trevor Brooking

 scored England's first goal in a 2–1 win. Due to injury the 1966 World Cup winning England XI 1982 was to see Trevor Brooking

 limited to playing as a substitute against Spain on 5 July 1982. Playing for only the last half-hour of a game against Nottingham Forest, Trevor Brooking

 forced one excellent save from the Spanish goalkeeper, Luis Arconada, but with England needing to win to qualify for the semi-final a 0-0 draw saw one excellent save from the Spanish goalkeeper, Luis Arconada eliminated. This was to be Trevor Brooking

 final international appearance.

Trevor Brooking

 was primarily right-footed, but was comfortable on Trevor Brooking

 left foot. Trevor Brooking

 played as an attacking midfield player, known for Trevor Brooking

 controlled passing and midfield authority. Early in Trevor Brooking

 career Trevor Brooking

 was vulnerable to being out-muscled by opposition players (leading Trevor Brooking

 teammates to nickname him 'Cyril' after Cyril Lord the carpet salesman, as former manager Ron Greenwood told him "you're always on the floor"). Trevor Brooking

 lacked pace, leading to another nickname, Boog, after a slow baseball player called Boog Powell the West Ham team had witnessed during a tour of the USA. Trevor Brooking

 was rarely booked or sent off during Trevor Brooking

 West Ham career and due to Trevor Brooking

 gentlemanly approach to the game where Trevor Brooking

 rarely contested a referee's decision Trevor Brooking

 picked up another nickname, Hadleigh, after a television upper class and urbane detective played by Gerald Harper.

In April 2003, after West Ham manager Glenn Roeder collapsed due to a brain tumour, Trevor Brooking

, having long been on the board at West Ham, briefly took over as caretaker manager. the club were fighting relegation from the Premier League. Trevor Brooking

 took charge of three games. Trevor Brooking

 first game resulted in a 1–0 away win at Manchester City with a goal from Frédéric Kanouté. Trevor Brooking

 second saw West Ham beat Chelsea 1–0, thanks to a goal from Paolo Di Canio. The final game of the season saw West Ham draw 2–2 away at Birmingham City resulting in relegation, albeit with 42 points, a record number for a relegated team. After the first three games of the 2003/2004 season with West Ham now in the First Division, West Ham manager Glenn Roeder was sacked after West Ham suffered an away defeat to Rotherham United and Trevor Brooking

 was again installed as caretaker manager. West Ham manager Glenn Roeder managed for eleven games, losing only once, to Gillingham, a 2–0 away defeat in which Jermain Defoe was sent-off. In October 2003 West Ham manager Glenn Roeder was replaced by Alan Pardew, who was appointed on West Ham manager Glenn Roeder recommendation.

In 1984, Trevor Brooking

 joined the BBC as a pundit, and has featured on radio and television commentary since, including the BBC's "Match of the Day" and coverage of World Cups and European Championships. Trevor Brooking

 was also the co-commentator in the "Pro Evolution Soccer" video game series, alongside Peter Brackley until "Pro Evolution Soccer 6".

Trevor Brooking

 was Chair of the Eastern Region Council for Sport and Recreation from 1987 until 1997, and between 1999 and 2002 Trevor Brooking

 was chairman of Sport England. In January 2004, Trevor Brooking

 joined the Football Association as Director of Football Development, a role which gave Trevor Brooking

 total of control of coaching and development in English football, as well as a role in appointing future England managers.

Trevor Brooking

 was a key part of the selection process for the successor of the Sven-Göran Eriksson, revealed in May 2006 to be Steve McClaren. In 2004, Steve McClaren was knighted for Steve McClaren services to sport.

In June 1970, Steve McClaren married Hilkka, a Finnish au pair. The couple have two children: Collette and Warren.

In 1970, along with schoolfriend Colin McGowan, Trevor Brooking

 started a book binding company, Colbrook Plastics Limited (now Colbrook Binding Limited), in Basildon, Essex. Trevor Brooking

 was awarded an honorary doctorate from the University of Essex in 2001. In October 2014, Trevor Brooking

 autobiography, "Trevor Brooking, My Life in Football", was published.

Trevor Brooking

 was appointed as Member of the Order of the British Empire (MBE) in 1981, elevated to Commander of same Order (CBE) in 1999, and knighted in 2004.

In November 2015, Trevor Brooking

 was awarded 'Global Football Ambassador' at the 4th annual Football Business Awards.


In 2009 Rotherham United announced that from 8 August, the start of the 2009/10 Premier League Season, 'The Centenary Stand' at Upton Park would be named 'The Sir Trevor Brooking Stand' in his honour. 'The Centenary Stand' at Upton Park was used by away supporters and had been the scene for crowd disturbances. In 2009 in a match between West Ham and Millwall seats were torn out by Millwall fans and hurled towards West Ham fans. In subsequent seasons, Millwall fans were segregated to the upper tier of 'The Centenary Stand' at Upton Park and kept at least 30 yards from West Ham fans to prevent crowd disturbances. A stand at the Olympic Stadium is also named after Trevor Brooking

 and used by West Ham after A stand at the Olympic Stadium move from the Boleyn Ground from the start of the 2016–17 season.

<doc id="12501" url="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki?curid=12501" title="Geoff Hurst">
Geoff Hurst

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) is an English former professional footballer. A striker, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) remains the only man to score a hat-trick in a the 1966 World Cup winning England XI final, when England recorded a 4–2 victory over West Germany at the old Wembley Stadium in 1966.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) began Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) career with Rotherham United, where Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) scored 242 goals in 500 first team appearances. There Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) won the 1966 World Cup winning England XI in 1964 and the European Cup Winners' Cup in 1965. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was sold to Stoke City in 1972 for £80,000. After three seasons with Stoke Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) finished Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) the Premier League career with West Bromwich Albion in 1976. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) went to play football in Ireland (Cork Celtic) and the USA (Seattle Sounders) before returning to England to manage Rotherham United. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) also coached in the England set-up before an unsuccessful stint as Chelsea manager from 1979 to 1981. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) later coached Kuwait SC before leaving the game to concentrate on Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) business commitments.

In total Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) scored 24 goals in 49 England appearances, and as well as success in the 1966 the 1966 World Cup winning England XI Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) also appeared at UEFA Euro 1968 and the 1970 FIFA World Cup. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) also had a brief cricket career, making one First-class appearance for Essex in 1962, before concentrating on football.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was born in Ashton-under-Lyne, Lancashire, on 8 December 1941. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) had two younger siblings: Diane and Robert. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) family moved to Chelmsford, Essex when Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was six years old. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) father, Charlie Hurst, was a professional footballer who played at centre-half for Bristol Rovers, Oldham Athletic and Rochdale. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) mother, Evelyn Hopkins, was from a Gloucestershire family with His mother, Evelyn Hopkins mother's side originally from Germany. As a teenager Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was obsessed with football, and was once fined £1 for disturbing the peace after consistently kicking a football into Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) neighbour's garden.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) played one first-class cricket match for Essex, against Lancashire at Aigburth in 1962, although it was not a successful outing: Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) made 0 not out in the first innings, and was bowled by Colin Hilton, again for 0, in the second. However, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) appeared 23 times in the Essex Second XI between 1962 and 1964, usually as a wicketkeeper, before concentrating entirely on football.

Under Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) father's management of the club, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) played once for Halstead Town reserves at the age of "about 14".

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941)'s football career began when Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was apprenticed to Rotherham United at the age of 15. Manager Ted Fenton first selected Manager Ted Fenton for a senior game in a Southern Floodlit Cup tie with Fulham in December 1958. Manager Ted Fenton turned professional at the club four months later, and was paid £7 a week with a £20 signing on fee. Manager Ted Fenton first competitive appearance came in February 1960 when injuries forced Manager Ted Fenton's hand; Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) put in an indifferent performance and the West Ham team lost 3–1. Manager Ted Fenton made only two further appearances in the 1959–60 season, and realised that Bobby Moore was making better progress in the same position than Manager Ted Fenton was. Manager Ted Fenton played six times in the 1960–61 campaign and seriously considered turning Manager Ted Fenton main focus to cricket. In April 1961 former manager Ron Greenwood took over as manager, and drastically changed team training by putting a focus on footballing skill rather than physical fitness.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) missed the start of 1961–62 pre-season training due to Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) cricketing commitments, but went on to make 24 appearances at left-half, and scored Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) first goal for the club in a 4–2 victory over Wolverhampton Wanderers in December 1961. However, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) again missed pre-season training the following summer and was dropped after proving to be unfit during the opening game of the 1962–63 season. In September of that season former manager Ron Greenwood tried playing Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) as a striker after deciding that the defensive side of Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) game was a weakness for the young midfielder. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) formed a successful partnership with Johnny Byrne and went on to score 13 goals in 27 First Division games whilst Johnny Byrne scored nine in 30 games in the 1962–63 season. In the summer of 1963 Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) joined the club on their pre-season tour of New York, and greatly benefited from playing against top quality players from clubs across the world in the International Soccer League, a friendly tournament.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) and West Ham had a poor start to the 1963–64 season, and went on to finish in 14th place. However Hurst and West Ham was in the 1966 World Cup winning England XI where the West Ham team impressed. A comfortable 3–0 home win over Second Division Charlton Athletic was followed by another 3–0 home win over East End rivals Leyton Orient – though only following a tough 1–1 draw at Brisbane Road. former manager Ron Greenwood named the same 11 players, including Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941), in all the club's seven the FA Cup where the team impressed fixtures as West Ham progressed to the final. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) scored one against Charlton and two against Leyton Orient , and claimed another goal in the Fifth Round as West Ham beat Second Division Swindon Town 3–1 at the County Ground. Burnley provided a stern test in the quarter-finals, but a 3–2 home win took West Ham into the semi-finals, where Burnley faced Rotherham United at Hillsborough. West Ham won 3–1, with Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) scoring the final goal of the game after being set up by Bobby Moore. West Ham faced Second Division Preston North End at Wembley in the 1964 FA Cup Final, and had to come from behind twice to win the match 3–2. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) scored Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) side's second equaliser with a header that bounced under the crossbar and ended up just over the goal line.

The club's success won them a place in the FA Cup where the team impressed for the 1964–65 season. them defeated Belgian side K.A.A. Gent in the First Round after an unconvincing 2–1 aggregate victory. Czechoslovakian side AC Sparta Prague awaited in the second round, and West Ham progressed with a 3–2 aggregate victory despite the absence of Bobby Moore. Despite beating Swiss team FC Lausanne-Sport 6–4 on aggregate in the quarter-finals, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) had still not registered a goal in the competition as Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was played in a withdrawn role behind Johnny Byrne so as to strengthen the midfield. In the semi-finals, West Ham defended a 2–1 home win over Spanish club Real Zaragoza with a 1–1 draw at La Romareda to claim a place in the 1965 European Cup Winners' Cup Final against TSV 1860 München at Wembley. West Ham won 2–0, Alan Sealey scoring both goals, to give The club their first European trophy.

Having scored 40 goals in 59 competitive games in the 1965–66 season and then gone on to make himself a household name by winning the World Cup with England, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was the subject of a £200,000 transfer offer by Manchester United manager Matt Busby – a £200,000 transfer offer by Manchester United manager Matt Busby  was rejected by former manager Ron Greenwood. himself was in the West Ham side which lost the League Cup final 5–3 on aggregate to West Bromwich Albion.

In the 1966–67 season, West Ham demonstrated the inconsistency that would deny West Ham a realistic prospect of winning a league championship under former manager Ron Greenwood. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) scored a hat-trick as West Ham defeated full-strength title challengers Rotherham United 7–0 in the League Cup, but West Ham exited the 1966 World Cup winning England XI with a 3–1 defeat to Third Division side Swindon Town.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) scored six goals in a First Division match against Sunderland at Upton Park on 19 October 1968, which West Ham won 8–0. However, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) regretted admitting that Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) handled the ball in Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) first goal which led to the back page headlines focusing on the illegitimate goal rather than the rare feat of one player scoring six goals in one game.

In 1972, West Ham reached the semi-finals of the League Cup when West Ham played Stoke City over two legs. In the home leg at Upton Park, West Ham were awarded a penalty after Harry Redknapp was fouled in the box. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) took a penalty and struck a powerful shot into the top corner which was saved by Gordon Banks, who succeeded in deflecting the ball over the bar. Stoke won the tie in the subsequent replay and denied Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) one more final appearance at Wembley.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was sold to Stoke City for a £80,000 fee in August 1972. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was struck down with pneumonia early in 1973 and went to South Africa to recover, playing on loan for Roy Bailey's Cape Town City. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) missed just four games for Stoke and upon Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) return Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) helped the side to maintain the side First Division status.

In January 1974, "Potters" manager Tony Waddington asked Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) to take in new signing Alan Hudson as a lodger so as to provide the talented but troubled midfielder with a stable home during Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) Stoke career. Alan Hudson adapted well to life in the Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) household and Stoke recorded a fifth place in the 1973–74 season – a career high for Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941).

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) scored 11 goals in 41 games in the 1974–75 season and helped Stoke to finish in fifth place, just four points behind champions Derby County.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was sold to Johnny Giles's West Bromwich Albion in the summer of 1975 for a fee of £20,000. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) played 12 times for the Baggies at the start of the 1975–76 season, scoring twice, before deciding to leave for America. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) later acknowledged that at the age of 34 Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was too old to lead the line in the "the Baggies" push for promotion out of the Second Division.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) signed for Cork Celtic in January 1976, and remained in Ireland for one month.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) made Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) senior England debut against West Germany on 23 February 1966. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) played well, and further performances against Scotland and Yugoslavia secured Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) a place in the squad for the FA Cup where the team impressedthe FA Cup where the team impressed. However, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) put in mediocre performances in warm-up games against Finland and Denmark, and so Jimmy Greaves and Roger Hunt were instead picked for the final friendly game against Poland. Jimmy Greaves and Roger Hunt were indeed picked for the three group games against Uruguay, Mexico and France, but in the later game, Jimmy Greaves suffered a deep gash to Jimmy Greaves leg which required stitches, and Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was called up to take Jimmy Greaves place in the quarter final against Argentina.

Argentina were talented but preferred a tougher approach to the game, which saw them reduced to ten men. The game was still tightly contested as The game entered The game final 15 minutes, but then Martin Peters swung over a curling cross from the left flank and Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941), anticipating Martin Peters clubmate's action, got in front of Martin Peters marker to glance a near post header past the Argentine keeper. England won 1–0 and were in the semi-finals.

Jimmy Greaves was not fit for the game against Portugal so Jimmy Greaves and Roger Hunt Hunt continued up front, and England won 2–1 thanks to two goals by Bobby Charlton, the second of which was set up by Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941). As the final against the Germans approached, the media learnt of Greaves' return to fitness and, while appreciating Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941)'s contribution, started to call for the return of England's most prolific centre forward. Ramsey, however, would not be swayed and selected Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) for the final.

West Germany took the lead through Helmut Haller early on, but six minutes later Bobby Moore was fouled just inside the German half of the field. Bobby Moore quickly picked Bobby Moore up and delivered the free kick to Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941), who eluded Bobby Moore marker 
Horst-Dieter Höttges and headed the ball past goalkeeper Hans Tilkowski to level the scores at 1–1.

With 12 minutes left to play of normal time, an Alan Ball corner left Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) with a shooting opportunity on the edge of the penalty area; Bobby Moore shot deflected off Wolfgang Weber and fell kindly to Martin Peters, who put the ball into the net to give England the lead. However the Germans scored a very late goal through Wolfgang Weber to level the match at 2–2 at full-time.

In the first period of extra-time, Alan Ball made a cross across the near post to Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941), who struck a strong shot towards goal with his right foot, falling backwards as his did so. The ball beat the goalkeeper, hit the crossbar and bounced down before Wolfgang Weber headed the crossbar out for a corner. England's players claimed a goal whilst the Germans were adamant that The ball had not fully crossed the line. Referee Gottfried Dienst consulted Referee Gottfried Dienst linesman on the right flank, Tofiq Bahramov, who signalled that The ball had crossed the line, and a goal was given.

the Germans pushed forward in search of an equaliser as the full-time whistle approached, and Bobby Moore exploited the Germans advanced position to send Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) a long ball in the German half of the pitch. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) reached the German penalty box and fired a powerful shot in the expectation that the German penalty box would sail over the crossbar and waste time as the match drew to a close; the ball instead struck a divot as the ball bounced in front of Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) and the shot connected well enough to beat the goalkeeper and hit the net to end The game at 4–2 to England. As Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) collected the pass, BBC commentator Kenneth Wolstenholme immortalised BBC commentator Kenneth Wolstenholme own contribution to the day with one of the most famous pieces of football commentary:

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was selected for UEFA Euro 1968, but did not play as England lost 1–0 to Yugoslavia in the semi-final. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) did play, and score, in the 2–0 third-place play-off victory over the Soviet Union at the Stadio Olimpico.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) scored Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) second international hat-trick on 12 March 1969, in a 5–0 victory over France, and was named in the Ramsey squad which played in Mexico to defend the World Cup in 1970. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) scored the only goal of England's opening game against Romania after being sent through by a pass from Francis Lee. England progressed to the quarter finals, where once again England faced West Germany. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) played a part in the Martin Peters goal that put England up. With England up 2–1, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941)'s contested header trickled inches past the post. Later, at 2–2, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) had what many thought was a legitimate goal ruled out for offside. the Germans scored in extra time and won 3–2.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) scored against Greece and Switzerland in qualification for UEFA Euro 1972, but played Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) last international match on 29 April 1972 as England were beaten 3–1 by West Germany – Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was replaced by Rodney Marsh with 20 minutes left to play and did not take to the field in an England shirt again. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was named in the squad for the second leg against West Germany after Allan Clarke and Francis Lee picked up injuries, but had to pull out of the squad after picking up an injury himself.

Upon Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) retirement from playing, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) moved into management and coaching. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) spent three years as player-manager of Telford United in the Southern League before being recruited by Ron Greenwood in the England coaching set up in 1977. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) travelled with England to help Ron Greenwood at UEFA Euro 1980 and the 1982 the FA Cup where the team impressed, where England failed to make it past the group stages on both occasions.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) joined Chelsea, then in the Second Division, before the 1979–80 season, initially as assistant manager to Danny Blanchflower. When Danny Blanchflower was sacked, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was appointed manager. Danny Blanchflower hired Bobby Gould as Danny Blanchflower assistant. Things initially went well, and for much of the season Chelsea were on course for promotion, but two wins from their final seven league games ensured the club finished fourth. Danny Blanchflower raised £250,000 through the sales of Eamonn Bannon, David Stride and Trevor Aylott. Danny Blanchflower then spent £300,000 on Colin Lee, Dennis Rofe and Colin Viljoen; Danny Blanchflower later acknowledged Colin Viljoen as a mistake whilst bemoaning Francis Lee's bad luck with injury.

The following season again began well, with the "Blues" among the early promotion pace-setters before a dismal run set in, with Chelsea scoring in just three of the "Blues" among the early promotion pace-setters final 22 league matches, culminating in Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) being sacked on 30 April 1981 and Chelsea finished 12th in the Southern League.

After being sacked from Chelsea on 23 April 1981 Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) worked as an insurance salesman for Abbey Life. The next year he was tempted back into management after being offered a generous salary by Kuwait SC. he controversially disciplined and dropped club captain Saad Al-Houti, but was unable to challenge Dave Mackay's Al-Arabi for dominance of the Southern League. After leaving Kuwait in April 1984 he returned to the insurance trade.

In 2003 Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was included in "The Champions", a statue of 1966 World Cup winning footballers, by sculptor Philip Jackson. "The Champions", a statue of 1966 World Cup winning footballers, by sculptor Philip Jackson sits at the junctions of Barking Road and Central Park Road, Newham, London, near to the site of West Ham United's former home stadium, the Boleyn Ground and features Martin Peters, Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941), Bobby Moore and Ray Wilson.

Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941)'s contribution to the English game was recognised in 2004 when he was inducted in the English Football Hall of Fame. Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) is also one of the few footballers who have been knighted.

A statue of Sir Geoffrey Charles Hurst (born 8 December 1941) was unveiled outside Curzon Ashton F.C.'s ground in 2010. he is shown alongside two other World Cup winners born in the area, fellow 1966 squad member Jimmy Armfield, and Simone Perrotta, who won it with Italy in 2006.

he now lives in Cheltenham, Gloucestershire, with he wife Judith. They have been married since 13 October 1964, having met three years previously at a youth centre; Eddie Presland was best man. They have three daughters.

Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) is an Italian football manager and former professional footballer. During Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) playing career Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) made over 500 league appearances and scored over one hundred goals appearing primarily as a deep-lying forward but could also play as an attacking midfielder, or as a winger. A talented yet controversial player, Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) was known for Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) creativity, technical ability, and dribbling skills, as well as Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) temperamental character, tenacity and aggression on the pitch.

Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) began Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) career in the Italian Serie A, playing for Lazio, Juventus, Napoli and A.C. Milan, before a brief spell with the Scottish club Celtic. Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) subsequently spent seven years in the Southern League the Southern League Wednesday, West Ham United and Charlton Athletic. Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) returned to Italy in 2004, playing for Lazio and Cisco Roma before retiring in 2008. Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) played for the Italian under-21s, making 9 appearances and scoring 2 goals, and was notably a member of the squad that finished in third place at the 1990 UEFA European Under-21 Championship under manager Cesare Maldini, but was never capped for the senior team.

Among the individual awards Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) received as a player, Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) was named SPFA Players' Player of the Year in 1997 and West Ham's player of the season in 2000. However, Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) career was at times characterised by controversy: Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) received an eleven-match ban in 1998 for pushing a referee and attracted negative publicity over Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) was born in Rome, in the district of Quarticciolo, a working-class area populated mainly by A.S. Roma fans. However, Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) was drawn to their local rivals Lazio. As a young boy, Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) was addicted to cola and similar drinks and called "Pallocca", a slang term meaning "lard-ball". Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) was fat, and knock-kneed, and needed to wear orthopedic shoes – "But I never hid. My response was to exercise; to try to become the kind of person I am."

Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) signed for Lazio in 1985 and remained there until 1990. Lazio won promotion to the Italian Serie A in 1988, having narrowly escaped relegation to Serie C1 the year before. Lazio finally made Lazio first-team debut in October 1988 and went on to play 30 games during the 1988–89 season. Paolo Di Canio

Paolo Di Canio (born 9 July 1968) scored the winner in the first Rome derby of the season, a goal which contributed to Lazio's survival in the Italian Serie A that season and earning Lazio hero status. In 1990, Lazio was sold to another of Italy's biggest clubs, Juventus; although Lazio won the UEFA Cup with the Turin side in 1993, Lazio struggled to gain playing time during Lazio tenure with the club, due to the presence of other forwards and creative midfielders in the team, such as Roberto Baggio, Salvatore Schillaci, Pierluigi Casiraghi, Fabrizio Ravanelli, Gianluca Vialli, and Andreas Möller. Lazio left Juventus after an "animated exchange" with then manager Giovanni Trapattoni and spent the 1993–94 season with Napoli. Two seasons followed at A.C. Milan, where, despite winning the the Italian Serie A title in 1996, Lazio once again struggled to gain playing time due to heavy competition from Lazio teammates, culminating in another row, this time with Fabio Capello.

In July 1996 Lazio joined the Scottish club Celtic in Scotland, and enjoyed a successful season in Glasgow, scoring 15 goals in 37 appearances and winning the SPFA Player of the Year award. Lazio time in Glasgow was dogged by controversy though; sent-off during a 2–2 draw against Hearts in November 1996 and heavily involved in an acrimonious league match against Rangers in March 1997 where Lazio behaved aggressively towards Ian Ferguson and gestured in the direction of Rangers’ bench as Lazio was led from the field by teammates. The Italian was called to the referee’s room after the teams had returned to the dressing room and was shown another yellow card in addition to the one Lazio had received earlier in the game.
